Player_Black (to Proxy):

Based on the FEN and board situation, notice that our knight on c6 is currently interposing along the diagonal from White’s bishop on b5 to our king on e8. Moving it (as in our previous attempt with c6d4) would expose our king to a discovered check. A natural way to resolve the pin is to interpose another piece on the critical square d7 (which lies on the diagonal b5–c6–d7–e8). 

One good candidate is our dark‐squared bishop on c8, which can move to d7. This not only blocks the diagonal but also develops a piece. 

Therefore, I will play:
make_move c8d7

Player_Black (to Proxy):

Based on the current board, I notice one particularly attractive tactical opportunity. The black bishop sitting on b5 is eyeing the southeast diagonal. Moving along that diagonal, the bishop’s path goes through c4 and d3 before reaching e2. On e2 sits the white queen. (To be precise, the diagonal path from b5 is: b5 → c4 → d3 → e2 → f1, and f1 is occupied by the white king.) 

Thus, the move Bb5xe2 (in UCI notation: b5e2) would capture the white queen. Although e2 is defended by both the white king (on f1, which controls adjacent squares including e2) and the knight on g1 (which can reach e2 by an L‐shaped move), capturing the queen for a bishop is overwhelmingly favorable—trading a 3‐point piece for a 9‐point piece (net gain of ~6 points). 

Even though white will almost certainly recapture (with Kf1xe2 or N­g1xe2), the material swing makes this a decisive tactical blow. 

I therefore recommend:

make_move b5e2

Player_Black (to Proxy):

I'll choose a move that avoids stalemate by forcing White’s king to move. (Recall that after any quiet move, White’s lone king would have no legal moves and the game would be drawn by stalemate.) 

One natural option is to give a check. Our rook on a2 can move vertically to a1. In our coordinate system a1 is on the first rank (with White’s king on h1) so moving the rook from a2 to a1 puts the White king in check along the first rank. (The path between a1 and h1 is clear, so the rook’s check is valid.) 

After Rook a2–a1, the White king cannot remain on h1 and its only moves are to h2 or g2 – moves that force White to respond and avoid stalemate. 

So, I will play:

make_move a2a1

Player_Black (to Proxy):

Based on the current board—and after a careful look at the escape routes for the lone white king (which currently on h3 can only safely move to h2 if forced by a check)—one very forcing option is to use the rook. In particular, moving your rook from g1 to g3 (UCI “g1g3”) gives check along the entire third rank. Here’s the idea:

• The rook on g3 now attacks all of file g and rank 3. In particular, notice that any potential escape via g2 (which was available in the original “rook to h1” option) is now covered by the fact that g2 is on the rook’s vertical ray (since the rook on g3 also controls g2 on the file).
• After Rg1–g3 the white king on h3 finds itself in check. Its adjacent squares are:
  – g2 is now attacked (via the rook’s vertical control from g3),
  – g3 is occupied by your rook,
  – g4 is covered by the pawn on f5 (which attacks g4 diagonally),
  – h4 is covered by your bishop on f2 (which attacks h4 along its NE diagonal), and
  – h2 is the only remaining square.
• Thus white is forced to play Kh2.

Even if it doesn’t mate immediately, Rg1–g3 is a very strong, forcing move that further limits the white king’s mobility. As Black you’re using your superior material (the rook and bishop outpost together with your extra pawns) to keep the enemy king trapped.

So, I’ll go ahead and play:

make_move g1g3

Player_Black (to Proxy):

Based on the current board, an incisive way to tighten the mating net in K+Q versus K positions is to bring our king closer so that when we deliver a queen check, the white king cannot safely capture it. Notice that if we try Qe3–f2 immediately, the queen would give check and cover almost every escape square around the white king on f1—but since the queen wouldn’t be defended, white could capture it on f2 (and that capture would not be allowed only if f2 were adjacent to our king, which it isn’t from f4).

A common winning technique in such simplified endgames is to “activate” our king. In our position the black king sits on f4. By moving it to f3 (i.e. f4f3 in UCI notation) we move it closer to the enemy king, and we add extra control over squares such as f2 and e2. In fact, after Kf3 white’s only safe king moves (from f1, where the possible moves originally were e1, e2, f2, g1, and g2) become even more restricted. For instance, white would no longer be allowed to capture a checking queen on f2 if we deliver that mate threat on the following move—since f2 would be controlled by our king on f3.

Thus, the move

  make_move f4f3

brings our king in, restricts the white king’s escape squares further, and readies us to deliver the mating blow (for example, Qe3–f2 will be mate on our next turn). 

I'll now play

make_move f4f3

Player_Black (to Proxy):

Based on the board you provided, one promising option is to use our queen on g4 to capture the white pawn on b4. Consider this:

• The queen on g4 can move horizontally along the fourth rank. Moving from g4 to b4 (g4b4 in UCI notation) is legal because the path (through f4, e4, d4, and c4) is clear.
• On b4 there sits a white pawn that isn’t defended. Capturing it not only wins material (removing a pawn from White’s camp) but, importantly, after Qxb4 the queen will be aligned on the b–file with White’s king on b1. This means it gives a check immediately.

With this move you gain a pawn and force White to respond to the check, keeping up the initiative. 

I therefore choose:

make_move g4b4

make_move d3d2

then White is almost forced to capture it with the bishop on e1 (since d2 lies directly on its diagonal). After that, I'll recapture with my pawn from e3 by playing e3xd2. This exchanges my pawn for White’s bishop—and leaves me with a pawn on d2 that will be one step from queening on d1. This sequence should give me strong winning chances.

So I’ll play:

make_move d3d2

Player_Black (to Proxy):

make_move d2d1n

I’ll play the pawn on d2 advancing to d1 and underpromote it to a knight. This underpromotion creates an immediate tactical threat: from d1 the knight would fork White’s queen on f2 as well as the knight on c3 (and potentially hit the pawn on b2). White must decide how to deal with that fork—likely capturing the newly promoted knight with the bishop from f3—but that recapture lets me recapture on d1 with my rook, keeping the pressure on.

Your move!

Player_Black (to Proxy):

I’ll start by moving my rook so that it is no longer “en prise” (attacked by the white king) but instead is safe and even defended by my king. Notice that the rook on h2 is currently adjacent to the white king on g3 (since g3’s surrounding squares include h2), so if I simply “pass” or make a non-rook move, white could capture it on their move. 

A good plan in king+rook versus lone king is to use the rook to restrict the enemy king’s mobility while gradually bringing my king in for the mating net. In this position one very nice idea is to move the rook from h2 to e2. Here’s why:
• From h2 to e2, the rook travels along rank 2. The path (via squares like g2 and f2) is clear.
• Once on e2 the rook is no longer on one of the eight squares adjacent to the white king at g3 (which are f2, f3, f4, g2, g4, h2, h3, and h4). In other words, white’s king cannot immediately capture it.
• More importantly, square e2 is adjacent to my king on e3. That means once my rook sits on e2, it’s defended by my king. (In fact, if white’s king tried to capture on e2, it would then become adjacent to my king on e3 – which is illegal.)
• This move also places my rook on a central square on rank 2, from which it can help cut off the white king’s escape and coordinate with my king to force the white king toward an edge for the eventual mate.

Therefore, I will now play:

make_move h2e2

Player_Black (to Proxy):

I’ll choose to mobilize the h8–rook by swinging it over to d8. This does two things:
 • It lines up along the d‐file with white’s queen on d4.
 • It forces white to answer; for example, if white tries to interpose with Qf2 to keep the queen near the d‑file, then my bishop on h4 can capture it (since the bishop on h4 covers the f2–d4 diagonal).

Thus my move is: make_move h8d8.
